Design is an essential part of the wealth generating process. It may not drive the wheels of capitalism, but it helps to ensure that they are designed to turn reliably and elegantly. Many designers historically have also considered it their job to prevent the wheels from running people over. During such occasions design has enhanced its intellectual and cultural role and responsibility. Design is perhaps rising to this challenge again, using the power of its imagination to envisage a more beautiful and usable world for all those who live within it.

## Conclusion

The previous chapter argued that the nature of design is defined by the evolving interrelationship between economy and culture. We have now taken this analysis further through an examination of the value of design to the key components of this system: enterprises, economies, consumers and society. Particular attention has been given to current developments that are transforming the nature of this system: new production technologies, new modes of consumption and new social values. The aims of this discussion have been to establish the importance of design to industrial society at a critical juncture in its development, to consider design's autonomous potential to influence that development, and to suggest its likely future nature and role. Along the way we have also been able to address some specific issues and problems pertaining to design's integration within British industry and economy.

The commercial value of design was established in terms of its contribution to costs and to the range of non-price factors that increasingly determine compctitiveness. Although anecdotal and case study evidence must be treated cautiously, there is a rising body of empirical research that substantiates the competitive value of engineering, product, graphic and environmental design. Further research is clearly required, especially in service industries, to quantify its contribution.

Much research has shown a continuing reluctance on the part of British industry to recognise design's potential value. The "three cultures of industry" introduced in the previous chapter - management, engineering design and industrial design  that prevent the fuller integration of design, are seen to be rooted in a number of cultural and economic factors. Britain's class structure, dominant antiindustrial culture and economic and cultural imperial legacies have been handicaps to its industrial modernisation. Britain appears to have a greater interest in its illustrious and partly fictionalised past, rather than in the urgent problems confronting it in the present or the future, as witnessed by current concerns that Britain in contrast to other countries is neglecting vital infrastructure developments such as optical fibre cabling.

However, some argue that design's problems have as much to do with designers failing to understand the needs of industry, the context of change and how to present their skills effectively. David Walker, for example, has suggested that "the egocentricity of designers, their antipathy to management and their context-resistant education"s2 has contributed to their marginalisation from the changes taking place in the industrial system. These points have much validity, although perhaps the harsh realities of recession and changes within education are making design more outward in orientation and less messianic in its self-image. However, such problems are more symptomatic of the historical forces that have marginalised design in British industrial culture.

While there is much theoretical and empirical evidence that innovation is related to economic growth, there is less recognition in Britain of the role played by design in the various phases of innovation. Especially in the field of consumer electronics, design is an important factor that transforms basic innovation into competitive products for world markets. Design takes on a new significance in the context of current developments within the system of production. The new industrial paradigm, characterised by new technologies, flexible production and diversifying markets, increases the requirements on design to feed this system with new product concepts, model variations, differentiated niche retailers, images for new communication media and lifestyle concepts.

Building on the concept of consumer culture introduced in the preus chapter ehavegued that des's l increasingly that of constructing and symbolising the lifestyle choices made by consumers and enhancing the pleasure derived from the act of consumption. We must, however, temper any notions of "consumer democracy" by the social polarisation created during the most recent phase of consumer society, which has swollen the large underclass of people excluded from consumerism and increasingly alienated from society as a whole. This constitutes one of the social problems that new initiatives from the design community are seeking to address.

Design's role as an intermediary between economy and culture means that its value can be measured not just in economic but in social terms as well. It has historically linked itself to progressive social movements, from William Morris through to the environmentalists of today. Ironically the products of some of these design movements, especially those seeking to revive craft methods, have been priced beyond the reach of ordinary consumers. They too can be interpreted as products of a reactionary anti-industrial culture that seeks to ignore the realities of the modern age, whether because of utopianism or a desire to restore the old order. Design's social relevance is most acute when, as in the case of modernism, it embraces technological change and new industrial systems, harnessing their potential power in responding to the changing needs and aspirations of the age. I

Design in the 1990s is at a critical juncture in its development. The new industrial paradigm offers great opportunities to fulfil needs demanded by our culture. Flexible production enables products to be tailored more easily to individual need and could thus provide better and cheaper goods for those with disabilities, for example. More symbolic and cultural diversity can be enabled. New information systems should allow designers and manufacturers to assess more easily the environmental impact of design and other production decisions. As the Little Rock agenda shows, design has a contribution to economic development, urban regeneration, multi-cultural diversity and the democratic process. A regrettable misconception of recent times is that design strategies to enhance economic development and those which champion social objectives are mutually exclusive. Just as utility cannot be dislocated from aesthetic so too in a civilised society, economic value cannot be divorced from social value in design.

The social responsibility of design is not just the concern of designers and governments. The analysis presented in this chapter has implications for the strategies of companies as well. They must ensure that design contributes to their competitiveness in as full a way as possible, which increasingly includes reflecting the cultural requirements and social values of their customers. The following chapter explores some of these issues in corporate design strategies.

## Summary

## The value of design to the enterprise

Design contributes to the competitiveness of enterprises by reducing manufacturing costs, improving quality and enhancing company image. Various research studies have demonstrated how design improves financial performance, raises market share and increases exports. In the service sector, corporate identity, information and environmental design have increased efficiencies and differentiated companies in the market.

There is much evidence to demonstrate that British industry is less committed to design than many of its competitors. This is one factor that accounts for the decline in Britain's manufacturing industry, and is the consequence of a number of cultural and economic factors. In the light of major industrial, economic and cultural transformations, an outdated view of design tends to dominate in Britain.

## The value of design to the national economy

Design plays a critical role in applying innovation and contributing to national economic growth. While in Britain there is an emphasis on basic research, the experience of Japan

demonstrates how national economies can prosper more through product innovation, design and marketing than fundamental R&D.

## The value of design after Henry

The development of a new technoeconomic paradigm - post-Fordism ncreases he flexibiliy manufacuring offers new opportunity for design variations and creates new patterns of consumption. Described by some as "the new economy of innovation", the value of design is greatly increased, acting as a bridge between new production opportunities and more diverse consumer needs.

## Values made visible

The new consumer culture that accompanies these new systems of production has increased the symbolic value of commodities relative to their functional or price value. Design is the process that adapts products and services to the lifestyle requirements of their consumers, thereby manifesting this symbolic value.

## Design and social values

Design reflects dominant social values although designers themselves retain some degree of autonomy in pursuing their own cultural interests by virtue of their class position. The history of design shows how the design community has allied itself to movements for progressive change. Design is already improving the environmental impact of some industries, and new initiatives are enabling design to respond appropriately to demographic change and cultural pluralism. Recent proposals from designers in the United States show how design can further economic development, urban renewal and democratic objectives. The commercial and social values of design are equally important in the changing context within which employers are operating.
